<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Global warming</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This page is about the current warming of the Earth's climate system. "<a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a>" can also refer to climate trends at any point in Earth's history. For other uses see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Global_warming_(disambiguation)" title="Global warming (disambiguation)">Global warming (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="805" data-file-width="951" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/320px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/480px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/640px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global mean surface-temperature change from 1880 to 2016, relative to the 1951–1980 mean. The black line is the global annual mean, and the red line is the five-year <a href="/wiki/Local_regression" title="Local regression">local regression</a> line. The blue <a href="/wiki/Error_bar" title="Error bar">uncertainty bars</a> show a 95% confidence interval.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Global warming</b>, also referred to as <b>climate change</b>, is the observed century-scale rise in the average temperature of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate system</a> and its related effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Multiple lines of scientific evidence show that the climate system is warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Many of the observed changes since the 1950s are unprecedented in the <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">instrumental temperature record</a> which extends back to the mid-19th century, and in <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">paleoclimate</a> <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy records</a> covering thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">Fifth Assessment Report</a> concluded that "It is <i>extremely likely</i> that human influence has been the <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">dominant cause</a> of the observed warming since the mid-20th century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The largest human influence has been the emission of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>. <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">Climate model</a> projections summarized in the report indicated that during the 21st century, the global surface temperature is likely to rise a further 0.3 to 1.7 °C (0.5 to 3.1 °F) in the lowest <a href="/wiki/Representative_Concentration_Pathways" title="Representative Concentration Pathways">emissions scenario</a>, and 2.6 to 4.8 °C (4.7 to 8.6 °F) in the highest emissions scenario.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> These findings have been recognized by the national science academies of the major industrialized nations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">are not disputed by any scientific body of national or international standing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Future climate change and associated impacts will differ from <a href="/wiki/Regional_effects_of_global_warming" title="Regional effects of global warming">region to region</a> around the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> Anticipated <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects</a> include increasing global temperatures, <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">rising sea levels</a>, changing <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">expansion of deserts</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Warming is expected to be greater over land than over the oceans and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline" title="Arctic sea ice decline">greatest in the Arctic</a>, with the continuing <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">retreat of glaciers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a>. Other likely changes include more frequent <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather</a> events such as <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heat waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a>, heavy <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfall</a> with <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a> and heavy <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snowfall</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_global_warming" title="Extinction risk from global warming">species extinctions</a> due to shifting temperature regimes. Effects significant to humans include the threat to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">food security</a> from decreasing crop yields and the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_migrant" title="Environmental migrant">abandonment of populated areas</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">rising sea levels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Because the climate system has a large "<a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity#Thermal_inertia" title="Volumetric heat capacity">inertia</a>" and greenhouse gases will remain in the atmosphere for a long time, many of these effects will persist for not only decades or centuries, but for tens of thousands of years to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Possible societal responses to global warming include <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">mitigation</a> by emissions reduction, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation" title="Climate change adaptation">adaptation</a> to its effects, building systems <a href="/wiki/Climate_resilience" title="Climate resilience">resilient</a> to its effects, and possible future <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a>. Most countries are parties to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> whose ultimate objective is to <a href="/wiki/Avoiding_Dangerous_Climate_Change" title="Avoiding Dangerous Climate Change">prevent dangerous anthropogenic climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Parties to the UNFCCC have agreed that deep cuts in emissions are required<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_2_degrees_celsius_target_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc_2_degrees_celsius_target-23">[22]</a></sup> and that global warming should be limited to well below 2.0 °C (3.6 °F) compared to pre-industrial levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[b]</a></sup> with efforts made to limit warming to 1.5 °C (2.7 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Public reactions to global warming and concern about its effects are also increasing. A global 2015 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> report showed that a <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> of 54% of all respondents asked consider it "a very serious problem". Significant regional differences exist, with <a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese</a> (whose economies are responsible for <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">the greatest annual CO<sub>2</sub> emissions</a>) among the least concerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_temperature_changes">Observed temperature changes</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">Instrumental temperature record</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:324px;max-width:324px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:322px;max-width:322px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png"><img alt="Map of temperature changes across the world" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="708" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png/320px-World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png/480px-World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png/640px-World_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.png 2x" width="320"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:322px;max-width:322px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg"><img alt="key to map of temperature changes" data-file-height="38" data-file-width="300" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg/320px-Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg/480px-Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg/640px-Key_to_world_map_showing_surface_temperature_trends_between_1950_and_2014.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">World map showing surface temperature trends (°C per decade) between 1950 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:322px;max-width:322px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="600" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png/320px-2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png/480px-2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/2000_Year_Temperature_Comparison.png 2x" width="320"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Two millennia of mean surface temperatures according to different reconstructions from <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">climate proxies</a>, each smoothed on a decadal scale, with the <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">instrumental temperature record</a> overlaid in black.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="713" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg/220px-Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg/330px-Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg/440px-Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annual_Global_Temperature_Anomalies_1950-2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a> graph of global annual temperature anomalies 1950–2012, showing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o_Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño Southern Oscillation">El Niño Southern Oscillation</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the period from 1880 to 2012, the global average (land and ocean) surface temperature has increased by 0.85 [0.65 to 1.06] °C, multiple independently produced datasets confirm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> In the period from 1906 to 2005, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_average_surface_temperature" title="Global average surface temperature">Earth's average surface temperature</a> rose by <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273890000000000♠</span>0.74<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.18 °C</span>. The rate of warming almost doubled in the last half of that period (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273279999999999♠</span>0.13<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.03 °C per decade</span>, against <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273219999999999♠</span>0.07<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.02 °C per decade</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> Although the popular press often reports the increase of the average near-surface atmospheric temperature as <i>the</i> measure of global warming, most of the additional energy stored in the climate system since 1970 has gone into the oceans. The rest has melted ice and warmed the continents and the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 1979, the average temperature of the lower <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> has increased between 0.12 and 0.135 °C (0.216 and 0.243 °F) per decade, <a href="/wiki/Satellite_temperature_measurements" title="Satellite temperature measurements">satellite temperature measurements</a> confirm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">Climate proxies</a> show the temperature to have been relatively stable over the <a href="/wiki/Temperature_record_of_the_past_1000_years" title="Temperature record of the past 1000 years">one or two thousand years</a> before 1850, with regionally varying fluctuations such as the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The warming evident in the instrumental temperature record is consistent with a wide range of observations, as documented by many independent scientific groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming-36">[33]</a></sup> Examples include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_sea_level_rise" title="Current sea level rise">sea level rise</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> widespread melting of snow and land ice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> increased <a href="/wiki/Ocean_heat_content" title="Ocean heat content">heat content of the oceans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming-36">[33]</a></sup> increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_humidity" title="Specific humidity">humidity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming-36">[33]</a></sup> and the earlier <a href="/wiki/Phenology" title="Phenology">timing</a> of spring events,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> e.g., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowering" title="Flowering">flowering</a> of plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> that these changes could have occurred by chance is virtually zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-kennedy_evidence_for_global_warming-36">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_trends_and_short-term_fluctuations">Regional trends and short-term fluctuations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:320px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:630px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg/630px--Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:630px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="34.68" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg/Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg.480p.webm" type="video/webm; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_anomalies_arranged_by_country_1900_-_2016.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Temperature anomalies arranged by country 1900–2016. Deviation from the 1951–1980 mean temperature. Visualization based on GISTEMP data.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Temperature increases vary a lot across the globe. Since 1979, land temperatures have increased about twice as fast as ocean temperatures (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273399999999999♠</span>0.25 °C per decade</span> against <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273279999999999♠</span>0.13 °C per decade</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> Ocean temperatures increase more slowly than land temperatures because of the larger effective <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a> of the oceans and because oceans lose more heat by evaporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">beginning of industrialisation</a> in the eighteenth century, the temperature difference between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemispheres_of_the_Earth" title="Hemispheres of the Earth">hemispheres</a> has increased due to melting of sea ice and snow in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup> In the past one hundred years, average arctic temperatures have been increasing at almost twice the rate of the rest of the world; however, arctic temperatures are also highly variable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> Although more greenhouse gases are emitted in the Northern than in the Southern Hemisphere, this fact does not contribute to the difference in warming because the major greenhouse gases persist long enough to diffuse within as well as between the hemispheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Climate_inertia#Thermal_inertia" title="Climate inertia">thermal inertia</a> of the oceans and the slow responses of other indirect effects occasion the climate to take centuries or longer to adjust to past changes in forcings. One <a href="/wiki/Climate_commitment" title="Climate commitment">climate commitment</a> study concluded that if greenhouse gases were stabilized at year 2000 levels, surface temperatures would still increase by about one-half degree Celsius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> and another found that if they were stabilized at 2005 levels, surface warming could exceed a whole degree Celsius. Some of this surface warming will be driven by past natural forcings which are still seeking <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget#Climate_forcings_and_global_warming" title="Earth's energy budget">equilibrium in the climate system</a>. One study using a highly simplified climate model indicates these past natural forcings may account for as much as 64% of the committed 2050 surface warming and their influence will fade with time compared to the human contribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Global temperature is subject to short-term fluctuations that overlay long-term trends and can temporarily mask them. The relative stability in surface temperature from 2002 to 2009, which has since been dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_hiatus" title="Global warming hiatus">global warming hiatus</a> by the media and some scientists,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> is an example of such an episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> 2015 updates to account for differing methods of measuring ocean surface temperature measurements show a positive trend over the recent decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warmest_years_vs._overall_trend">Warmest years vs. overall trend</span></h3>
<p>Sixteen of the seventeen warmest years on record have occurred since 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> While record-breaking years attract considerable public interest, individual years are less significant than the overall trend. Some climatologists have criticized the attention that the popular press gives to "warmest year" statistics. In particular, ocean oscillations such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o_Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño Southern Oscillation">El Niño Southern Oscillation</a> (ENSO) can cause temperatures of a given year to be abnormally warm or cold for reasons unrelated to the overall trend of climate change. <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Schmidt" title="Gavin Schmidt">Gavin Schmidt</a> stated "the long-term trends or the expected sequence of records are far more important than whether any single year is a record or not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gavin_Schmidt_201501_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gavin_Schmidt_201501-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Initial_causes_of_temperature_changes_(external_forcings)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_causes_of_temperature_changes_.28external_forcings.29">Initial causes of temperature changes (external forcings)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="600" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png/300px-Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png/450px-Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1c/Carbon_Dioxide_400kyr.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations over the last 400,000 years.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect.svg"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="750" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/300px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/450px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/600px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Greenhouse effect schematic showing energy flows between space, the atmosphere, and Earth's surface. Energy exchanges are expressed in watts per square metre (W/m<sup>2</sup>).</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">Attribution of recent climate change</a></div>
<p>By itself, the climate system may generate random changes in global temperatures for years to decades at a time, but long-term changes emanate only from so-called <i>external forcings.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> These forcings are "external" to the climate system, but not necessarily external to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> Examples of external forcings include changes in the composition of the atmosphere (e.g., increased concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>), <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">solar luminosity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> eruptions, and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_forcing" title="Orbital forcing">variations in Earth's orbit</a> around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hegerl-2001_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hegerl-2001-59">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gases">Greenhouse gases</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">Greenhouse effect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget" title="Earth's energy budget">Earth's energy budget</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_climate_change_science" title="History of climate change science">History of climate change science</a></div>
<p>The greenhouse effect is the process by which <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation by gases in a planet's <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> warm its lower atmosphere and surface. It was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Fourier" title="Joseph Fourier">Joseph Fourier</a> in 1824, discovered in 1860 by <a href="/wiki/John_Tyndall" title="John Tyndall">John Tyndall</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> was first investigated quantitatively by <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a> in 1896,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> and its scientific description was developed in the 1930s through 1960s by <a href="/wiki/Guy_Stewart_Callendar" title="Guy Stewart Callendar">Guy Stewart Callendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector,_in_2010_(color)_png.png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="1200" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/220px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/330px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/440px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Annual world greenhouse gas emissions, in 2010, by sector.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="738" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg/220px-Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg/330px-Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg/440px-Percentage_share_of_global_cumulative_energy-related_carbon_dioxide_emissions_between_1751_and_2012_across_different_regions.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Percentage share of global cumulative energy-related CO<sub>2</sub> emissions between 1751 and 2012 across different regions.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On Earth, an atmosphere containing naturally occurring amounts of greenhouse gases causes air temperature near the surface to be about 33 °C (59 °F) warmer than it would be in their absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[d]</a></sup> Without the Earth's atmosphere, the Earth's average temperature would be well below the freezing temperature of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue-66">[62]</a></sup> The major greenhouse gases are <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapour</a>, which causes about 36–70% of the greenhouse effect; <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>), which causes 9–26%; <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), which causes 4–9%; and <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (O<sub>3</sub>), which causes 3–7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup> Clouds also affect the radiation balance through <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forcing" title="Cloud forcing">cloud forcings</a> similar to greenhouse gases.</p>
<p>Human activity since the Industrial Revolution has increased the amount of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere, leading to increased <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> from CO<sub>2</sub>, methane, <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone" title="Tropospheric ozone">tropospheric ozone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>. According to work published in 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gas">concentrations</a> of CO<sub>2</sub> and methane had increased by 36% and 148% respectively since 1750.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> These levels are much higher than at any time during the last 800,000 years, the period for which reliable data has been extracted from <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup> Less direct geological evidence indicates that CO<sub>2</sub> values higher than this were last seen about 20 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuel</a> burning has produced about three-quarters of the increase in CO<sub>2</sub> from human activity over the past 20 years. The rest of this increase is caused mostly by changes in land-use, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_and_climate_change" title="Deforestation and climate change">particularly deforestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> Another significant non-fuel source of anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions is the <a href="/wiki/Calcination" title="Calcination">calcination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> for <a href="/wiki/Clinker_(cement)" title="Clinker (cement)">clinker</a> production, a chemical process which releases CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> Estimates of global CO<sub>2</sub> emissions in 2011 from fossil fuel combustion, including cement production and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_flaring" title="Gas flaring">gas flaring</a>, was 34.8 billion tonnes (9.5 ± 0.5 PgC), an increase of 54% above emissions in 1990. Coal burning was responsible for 43% of the total emissions, oil 34%, gas 18%, cement 4.9% and gas flaring 0.7%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2013, it was reported that readings for CO<sub>2</sub> taken at the world's primary benchmark site in <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Loa_Observatory" title="Mauna Loa Observatory">Mauna Loa</a> surpassed 400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>. According to professor <a href="/wiki/Brian_Hoskins" title="Brian Hoskins">Brian Hoskins</a>, this is likely the first time CO<sub>2</sub> levels have been this high for about 4.5 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup> Monthly global CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations exceeded 400 ppm in March 2015, probably for the first time in several million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup> On 12 November 2015, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA scientists</a> reported that human-made carbon dioxide <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">continues to increase</a> above levels not seen in hundreds of thousands of years; currently, about half of the carbon dioxide released from the burning of fossil fuels is not absorbed by vegetation and the oceans and remains in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112-ab_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112-ab-82">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="540" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/220px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/330px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/440px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a> by country.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Over the last three decades of the twentieth century, <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> per capita and <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> were the main drivers of increases in greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> emissions are continuing to rise due to the burning of fossil fuels and land-use change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc2008_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrc2008-84">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank2010_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank2010-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:71</sup> Emissions can be <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Regional_and_national_attribution_of_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">attributed to different regions</a>. Attributions of emissions due to land-use change are subject to considerable uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-liverman-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:289</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change_scenario#Emissions_scenarios" title="Climate change scenario">Emissions scenarios</a>, estimates of changes in future emission levels of greenhouse gases, have been projected that depend upon uncertain economic, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technological</a>, and natural developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup> In most scenarios, emissions continue to rise over the century, while in a few, emissions are reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> Fossil fuel reserves are abundant, and will not limit carbon emissions in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup> Emission scenarios, combined with modelling of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, have been used to produce estimates of how atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases might change in the future. Using the six IPCC <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios" title="Special Report on Emissions Scenarios">SRES</a> "marker" scenarios, models suggest that by the year 2100, the atmospheric concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> could range between 541 and 970 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup> This is 90–250% above the concentration in the year 1750.</p>
<p>The popular media and the public often confuse global warming with <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a>, i.e., the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">stratospheric ozone (e.g., the ozone layer)</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup> Although there are a few <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion#Ozone_depletion_and_global_warming" title="Ozone depletion">areas of linkage</a>, the relationship between the two is not strong. Reduced stratospheric ozone has had a slight cooling influence on surface temperatures, while increased tropospheric ozone has had a somewhat larger warming effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerosols_and_soot">Aerosols and soot</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3937" data-file-width="4502" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg/220px-ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg/330px-ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg/440px-ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ShipTracks_MODIS_2005may11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ship_tracks" title="Ship tracks">Ship tracks</a> can be seen as lines in these clouds over the Atlantic Ocean on the east coast of the United States. Atmospheric particles from these and other sources could have a large effect on climate through the aerosol indirect effect.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>, a gradual reduction in the amount of global direct <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">irradiance</a> at the Earth's surface, was observed from 1961 until at least 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Particulates#Climate_effects" title="Particulates">Solid and liquid particles</a> known as <i>aerosols</i>, produced by volcanoes and human-made <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a>, are thought to be the main cause of this dimming. They exert a cooling effect by increasing the reflection of incoming sunlight. The effects of the products of fossil fuel combustion – CO<sub>2</sub> and aerosols – have partially offset one another in recent decades, so that net warming has been due to the increase in non-CO<sub>2</sub> greenhouse gases such as methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10944197_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10944197-97">[93]</a></sup> Radiative forcing due to aerosols is temporally limited due to the processes that remove aerosols from the atmosphere. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wet_deposition" title="Wet deposition">Removal by clouds and precipitation</a> gives tropospheric aerosols an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_lifetime" title="Atmospheric lifetime">atmospheric lifetime</a> of only about a week, while <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratospheric</a> aerosols can remain for a few years. Carbon dioxide has a lifetime of a century or more, and as such, changes in aerosols will only delay climate changes due to carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_carbon" title="Black carbon">Black carbon</a> is second only to carbon dioxide for its contribution to global warming (contribution being estimated at 17 to 20%, whereas carbon dioxide contributes 40 to 45% to global warming<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to their direct effect by scattering and absorbing solar radiation, aerosols have indirect effects on the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget" title="Earth's energy budget">Earth's radiation budget</a>. Sulfate aerosols act as <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">cloud condensation nuclei</a> and thus lead to clouds that have more and smaller cloud droplets. These clouds reflect solar radiation more efficiently than clouds with fewer and larger droplets, a phenomenon known as the <a href="/wiki/Twomey_effect" title="Twomey effect">Twomey effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> This effect also causes droplets to be of more uniform size, which reduces <a href="/wiki/Cloud_physics#Collision-coalescence" title="Cloud physics">growth of raindrops</a> and makes the cloud more reflective to incoming sunlight, known as the <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_effect" title="Albrecht effect">Albrecht effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> Indirect effects are most noticeable in marine stratiform clouds, and have very little radiative effect on convective clouds. Indirect effects of aerosols represent the largest uncertainty in radiative forcing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">Soot</a> may either cool or warm Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate system</a>, depending on whether it is airborne or deposited. Atmospheric soot directly absorbs solar radiation, which heats the atmosphere and cools the surface. In isolated areas with high soot production, such as rural India, as much as 50% of surface warming due to greenhouse gases may be masked by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_brown_cloud" title="Atmospheric brown cloud">atmospheric brown clouds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup> When deposited, especially on glaciers or on ice in arctic regions, the lower surface <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> can also directly heat the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> The influences of atmospheric particles, including black carbon, are most pronounced in the tropics and sub-tropics, particularly in Asia, while the effects of greenhouse gases are dominant in the extratropics and southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers,_1975-2013.png"><img alt="Refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1746" data-file-width="2318" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png/220px-Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png/330px-Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png/440px-Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers%2C_1975-2013.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Changes_in_total_solar_irradiance_and_monthly_sunspot_numbers,_1975-2013.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_solar_irradiance" title="Total solar irradiance">total solar irradiance</a> (TSI) and monthly <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspot</a> numbers since the mid-1970s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/220px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/330px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/440px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Contribution of natural factors and human activities to <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_radiative_forcing_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_radiative_forcing-108">[104]</a></sup> Radiative forcing values are for the year 2005, relative to the pre-industrial era (1750).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_radiative_forcing_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_radiative_forcing-108">[104]</a></sup> The contribution of solar irradiance to radiative forcing is 5% the value of the combined radiative forcing due to increases in the atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">methane</a> and nitrous oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_solar_activity_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa_solar_activity-109">[105]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_activity">Solar activity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_activity_and_climate" title="Solar activity and climate">Solar activity and climate</a></div>
<p>Since 1978, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar irradiance</a> has been measured by <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> These measurements indicate that the Sun's radiative output has not increased since then, so the warming that occurred in the past 40 years cannot be attributed to an increase in solar energy reaching the Earth.</p>
<p>Climate models have been used to examine the role of the Sun in recent climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup> Models are unable to reproduce the rapid warming observed in recent decades when only taking into account variations in solar output and volcanic activity. Models are, however, able to simulate the observed 20th century changes in temperature when they include all of the most important external forcings, consisting of both human influences and natural forcings.</p>
<p>Another line of evidence for the Sun's non-attributability is the differing temperature changes at different levels in the Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup> According to basic physical principles, the greenhouse effect produces warming of the lower atmosphere (the troposphere), but cooling of the upper atmosphere (the stratosphere).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randel2009_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randel2009-114">[110]</a></sup> If solar variations were responsible for the observed warming, warming of both the troposphere and the stratosphere would be expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Variations_in_Earth's_orbit"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_in_Earth.27s_orbit">Variations in Earth's orbit</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a></div>
<p>The tilt of the Earth’s axis and the shape of its orbit around the Sun vary slowly over tens of thousands of years. This changes climate by changing the seasonal and latitudinal distribution of incoming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">solar energy</a> at Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup> During the last few thousand years, this phenomenon contributed to a slow cooling trend at high latitudes of the Northern Hemisphere during summer, a trend that was reversed by greenhouse-gas-induced warming during the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup> Orbital cycles favorable for glaciation are not expected within the next 50,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feedback">Feedback</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_feedback" title="Climate change feedback">Climate change feedback</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_sensitivity" title="Climate sensitivity">Climate sensitivity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_the_edge_of_the_ice_in_Nunavut_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sea ice, shown here in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, in northern Canada, reflects more sunshine, while open ocean absorbs more, accelerating melting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The climate system includes a range of <i><a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">feedbacks</a></i>, which alter the response of the system to changes in external forcings. Positive feedbacks increase the response of the climate system to an initial forcing, while negative feedbacks reduce it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jackson_feedbacks_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-jackson_feedbacks-123">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are a range of feedbacks in the climate system, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_vapor_feedback" title="Water vapor feedback">water vapour</a>, changes in <a href="/wiki/Ice-albedo_feedback" title="Ice-albedo feedback">ice-albedo</a> (snow and ice cover affect how much the Earth's surface absorbs or reflects incoming sunlight), clouds, and changes in the Earth's carbon cycle (e.g., the release of carbon from <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup> The main negative feedback is the energy the Earth's surface <a href="/wiki/Radiative_cooling" title="Radiative cooling">radiates</a> into space as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup> According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stefan-Boltzmann_law" title="Stefan-Boltzmann law">Stefan-Boltzmann law</a>, if the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">absolute temperature</a> (as measured in <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>) doubles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[e]</a></sup> radiated energy increases by a factor of 16 (2 to the 4th power).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Feedbacks are an important factor in determining the sensitivity of the climate system to increased atmospheric greenhouse gas concentrations. Other factors being equal, a higher <i><a href="/wiki/Climate_sensitivity" title="Climate sensitivity">climate sensitivity</a></i> means that more warming will occur for a given increase in greenhouse gas forcing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup> Uncertainty over the effect of feedbacks is a major reason why different climate models project different magnitudes of warming for a given forcing scenario. More research is needed to understand the role of clouds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jackson_feedbacks_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-jackson_feedbacks-123">[119]</a></sup> and carbon cycle feedbacks in climate projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>The IPCC projections previously mentioned span the "likely" range (greater than 66% probability, based on expert judgement)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC-Def-Of-Certainty_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-Def-Of-Certainty-130">[125]</a></sup> for the selected emissions scenarios. However, the IPCC's projections do not reflect the full range of uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections-131">[126]</a></sup> The lower end of the "likely" range appears to be better constrained than the upper end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections-131">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_models">Climate models</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Predictions.png"><img alt="refer to caption" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="528" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Global_Warming_Predictions.png/300px-Global_Warming_Predictions.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Global_Warming_Predictions.png/450px-Global_Warming_Predictions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Global_Warming_Predictions.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Calculations of global warming prepared in or before 2001 from a range of climate models under the <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios" title="Special Report on Emissions Scenarios">SRES</a> A2 emissions scenario, which assumes no action is taken to reduce emissions and regionally divided economic development.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century,_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" data-file-height="1507" data-file-width="2811" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png/300px-Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png/450px-Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png/600px-Projected_change_in_annual_mean_surface_air_temperature_from_the_late_20th_century_to_the_middle_21st_century%2C_based_on_SRES_emissions_scenario_A1B.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Projected change in annual mean surface air temperature from the late 20th century to the middle 21st century, based on a medium <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_global_warming#Emissions_scenarios" title="Economics of global warming">emissions scenario</a> (SRES A1B).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> This scenario assumes that no future policies are adopted to limit greenhouse gas emissions. Image credit: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">NOAA</a> <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_Fluid_Dynamics_Laboratory" title="Geophysical Fluid Dynamics Laboratory">GFDL</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">Global climate model</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">climate model</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_models" title="Mathematical models">representation</a> of the physical, chemical and biological processes that affect the climate system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup> Such models are based on scientific disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a> as well as physical processes such as <a href="/wiki/Radiative_transfer" title="Radiative transfer">radiative transfer</a>. The models may be used to predict a range of variables such as local air movement, temperature, clouds, and other atmospheric properties; ocean temperature, <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salt content</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_circulation" title="Ocean circulation">circulation</a>; ice cover on land and sea; the transfer of heat and moisture from soil and vegetation to the atmosphere; and chemical and biological processes, among others.</p>
<p>Although researchers attempt to include as many processes as possible, simplifications of the actual climate system are inevitable because of the constraints of available computer power and limitations in knowledge of the climate system. Results from models can also vary due to different greenhouse gas inputs and the model's climate sensitivity. For example, the uncertainty in IPCC's 2007 projections is caused by (1) the use of multiple models<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections-131">[126]</a></sup> with differing sensitivity to greenhouse gas concentrations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> (2) the use of differing estimates of humanity's future greenhouse gas emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_2007_global_warming_projections-131">[126]</a></sup> (3) any additional emissions from climate feedbacks that were not included in the models IPCC used to prepare its report, i.e., greenhouse gas releases from permafrost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSDIC_permafrost_study_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSDIC_permafrost_study-136">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The models do not assume the climate will warm due to increasing levels of greenhouse gases. Instead the models predict how greenhouse gases will interact with radiative transfer and other physical processes. Warming or cooling is thus a result, not an assumption, of the models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clouds and their effects are especially difficult to predict. Improving the models' representation of clouds is therefore an important topic in current research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup> Another prominent research topic is expanding and improving representations of the carbon cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Models are also used to help investigate the <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">causes of recent climate change</a> by comparing the observed changes to those that the models project from various natural and human causes. Although these models do not unambiguously attribute the warming that occurred from approximately 1910 to 1945 to either natural variation or human effects, they do indicate that the warming since 1970 is dominated by anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hegerl-2001_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hegerl-2001-59">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The physical realism of models is tested by examining their ability to simulate contemporary or past climates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup> Climate models produce a good match to observations of global temperature changes over the last century, but do not simulate all aspects of climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup> Not all <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of global warming</a> are accurately predicted by the climate models used by the IPCC. Observed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_shrinkage" title="Arctic shrinkage">Arctic shrinkage</a> has been faster than that predicted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup> Precipitation increased proportionally to atmospheric humidity, and hence significantly faster than global climate models predict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup> Since 1990, sea level has also risen considerably faster than models predicted it would.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_and_expected_environmental_effects">Observed and expected environmental effects</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">Effects of global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._(2012).png"><img alt="Refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="761" data-file-width="1355" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png/350px-Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png/525px-Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png/700px-Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._%282012%29.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projections_of_global_mean_sea_level_rise_by_Parris_et_al._(2012).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Projections of global mean sea level rise by Parris and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parris_sea_level_rise_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-parris_sea_level_rise-148">[143]</a></sup> Probabilities have not been assigned to these projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup> Therefore, none of these projections should be interpreted as a "best estimate" of future sea level rise. Image credit: NOAA.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Anthropogenic forcing has likely contributed to some of the observed changes, including sea level rise, changes in climate <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extremes</a> (such as the number of warm and cold days), <a href="/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline" title="Arctic sea ice decline">declines in Arctic sea ice</a> extent, <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">glacier retreat</a>, and greening of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup> and snow cover<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup> are projected to continue their widespread retreat. Projections of declines in Arctic sea ice vary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup> Recent projections suggest that Arctic summers could be ice-free (defined as ice extent less than 1 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_km" title="Square km">square km</a>) as early as 2025–2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Detection" is the process of demonstrating that climate has changed in some defined <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistical</a> sense, without providing a reason for that change. Detection does not imply attribution of the detected change to a particular cause. "Attribution" of causes of climate change is the process of establishing the most likely causes for the detected change with some defined level of confidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup> Detection and attribution may also be applied to observed changes in physical, ecological and social systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extreme_weather">Extreme weather</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">Extreme weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_impacts_of_climate_change#Extreme_events" title="Physical impacts of climate change">Physical impacts of climate change § Extreme events</a></div>
<p>Changes in regional climate are expected to include greater warming over land, with most warming at high northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a>, and least warming over the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> and parts of the North Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_SYR_2007_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_SYR_2007-159">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Future changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> are expected to follow existing trends, with reduced precipitation over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> land areas, and increased precipitation at subpolar latitudes and some <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial</a> regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup> Projections suggest a probable increase in the frequency and severity of some extreme weather events, such as <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heat waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2015 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Climate_Change" title="Nature Climate Change">Nature Climate Change</a></i>, states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>About 18% of the moderate daily precipitation extremes over land are attributable to the observed temperature increase since pre-industrial times, which in turn primarily results from human influence. For 2 °C of warming the fraction of precipitation extremes attributable to human influence rises to about 40%. Likewise, today about 75% of the moderate daily hot extremes over land are attributable to warming. It is the most rare and extreme events for which the largest fraction is anthropogenic, and that contribution increases nonlinearly with further warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT42715_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT42715-162">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureCC42715_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatureCC42715-163">[158]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Data analysis of extreme events from 1960 until 2010 suggests that droughts and heat waves appear simultaneously with increased frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> Extremely wet or dry events within the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> period have increased since 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level_rise">Sea level rise</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
