<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="2048" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/250px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/375px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg/500px-6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:6m_Sea_Level_Rise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Earth with a six-metre sea level rise represented in red.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Sea level rise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">Retreat of glaciers since 1850</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png"><img alt="refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="658" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/250px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/375px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/500px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sparse records indicate that glaciers have been retreating since the early 1800s. In the 1950s measurements began that allow the monitoring of glacial mass balance, reported to the <a href="/wiki/World_Glacier_Monitoring_Service" title="World Glacier Monitoring Service">World Glacier Monitoring Service</a> (WGMS) and the <a href="/wiki/National_Snow_and_Ice_Data_Center" title="National Snow and Ice Data Center">National Snow and Ice Data Center</a> (NSIDC).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The sea level rise since 1993 has been estimated to have been on average 2.6 mm and 2.9 mm per year ± 0.4 mm. Additionally, sea level rise has accelerated from 1995 to 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> Over the 21st century, the IPCC projects for a high emissions scenario, that global mean <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level</a> could rise by 52–98 cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_SLR2013_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_SLR2013-167">[162]</a></sup> The IPCC's projections are conservative, and may underestimate future sea level rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup> Other estimates suggest that for the same period, global mean sea level could rise by 0.2 to 2.0 m (0.7–6.6 ft), relative to mean sea level in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parris_sea_level_rise_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-parris_sea_level_rise-148">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Widespread <a href="/wiki/Coastal_flood" title="Coastal flood">coastal flooding</a> would be expected if several degrees of warming is sustained for millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-169">[164]</a></sup> For example, sustained global warming of more than 2 °C (relative to pre-industrial levels) could lead to eventual sea level rise of around 1 to 4 m due to thermal expansion of sea water and the melting of glaciers and small <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-169">[164]</a></sup> Melting of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a> could contribute an additional 4 to 7.5 m over many thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_long_term_slr-169">[164]</a></sup> It has been estimated that we are already committed to a sea-level rise of approximately 2.3 metres for each degree of temperature rise within the next 2,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warming beyond the 2 °C target would potentially lead to rates of sea-level rise dominated by ice loss from <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Continued CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from fossil sources could cause additional tens of metres of sea level rise, over the next millennia and eventually ultimately eliminate the entire Antarctic ice sheet, causing about 58 metres of sea level rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_systems">Ecological systems</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">Climate change and ecosystems</a></div>
<p>In terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, the earlier timing of spring events, as well as poleward and upward shifts in plant and animal ranges, have been linked with high confidence to recent warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc.ch_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc.ch-172">[167]</a></sup> Future climate change is expected to affect particular ecosystems, including <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangroves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_SYR_2007_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_SYR_2007-159">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">caves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[168]</a></sup> It is expected that most ecosystems will be affected by higher atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels, combined with higher global temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[169]</a></sup> Overall, it is expected that climate change will result in the <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> of many species and reduced diversity of ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Increases in atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations have led to an increase in <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncadac_2013_climate_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncadac_2013_climate-176">[171]</a></sup> Dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> increases ocean acidity, measured by lower <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncadac_2013_climate_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncadac_2013_climate-176">[171]</a></sup> Between 1750 and 2000, surface-ocean pH has decreased by ≈0.1, from ≈8.2 to ≈8.1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeebe_ocean_acidification_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeebe_ocean_acidification-177">[172]</a></sup> Surface-ocean pH has probably not been below ≈8.1 during the past 2 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeebe_ocean_acidification_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-zeebe_ocean_acidification-177">[172]</a></sup> Projections suggest that surface-ocean pH could decrease by an additional 0.3–0.4 units by 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[173]</a></sup> Future ocean acidification could threaten <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_with_coral_reefs" title="Environmental issues with coral reefs">coral reefs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protected_species" title="Protected species">protected species</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> of value to society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncadac_2013_climate_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-ncadac_2013_climate-176">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_deoxygenation" title="Ocean deoxygenation">Ocean deoxygenation</a> is projected to increase <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxia</a> by 10%, and triple suboxic waters (oxygen concentrations 98% less than the mean surface concentrations), for each 1 °C of upper ocean warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_effects">Long-term effects</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long-term_effects_of_global_warming" title="Long-term effects of global warming">Long-term effects of global warming</a></div>
<p>On the timescale of centuries to millennia, the magnitude of global warming will be determined primarily by anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup> This is due to carbon dioxide's very long lifetime in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stabilizing the global average temperature would require large reductions in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup> as well as reductions in emissions of other greenhouse gases such as methane and nitrous oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-3"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[177]</a></sup> Emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> would need to be reduced by more than 80% relative to their peak level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-4"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup> Even if this were achieved, global average temperatures would remain close to their highest level for many centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long-term_effects_of_global_warming_181-5"><a href="#cite_note-long-term_effects_of_global_warming-181">[176]</a></sup> As of 2016, emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> from burning fossil fuels had stopped increasing, but <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a> reports they need to be "reduced to have a real impact on climate change". Meanwhile, this greenhouse gas continues to accumulate in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup> In that context, the <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">New York Times</a> reported that scientific installations analyzing oceanic air detected the excess carbon dioxide in the atmosphere "rose at the highest rate on record in 2015 and 2016."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-184">[179]</a></sup> It hs been suggested that this rise in CO<sub>2</sub> levels is the result of changing absorption patterns of the ocean and land surface in that they may have reached the limit of their ability to absorb carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-184">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also, CO<sub>2</sub> is not the only factor driving climate change. Concentrations of atmospheric methane, another greenhouse gas, rose dramatically between 2006–2016 for unknown reasons. This undermines efforts to combat global warming and there is a risk of an uncontrollable <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Long-term effects also include a response from the Earth's crust, due to ice melting and deglaciation, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">post-glacial rebound</a>, when land masses are no longer depressed by the weight of ice. This could lead to <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a> and increased seismic and volcanic activities. Tsunamis could be generated by submarine landslides caused by warmer ocean water thawing ocean-floor permafrost or releasing <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrate" title="Clathrate hydrate">gas hydrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup> Some world regions, such as the French Alps, already show signs of an increase in landslide frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large-scale_and_abrupt_impacts">Large-scale and abrupt impacts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change" title="Abrupt climate change">Abrupt climate change</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cold_blob_(North_Atlantic)" title="Cold blob (North Atlantic)">Cold blob (North Atlantic)</a></div>
<p>Climate change could result in global, large-scale changes in <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">social systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup> Examples include the possibility for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Meridional_Overturning_Circulation" title="Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation">Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation</a> to slow- or shutdown, which in the instance of a shutdown would change weather in Europe and North America considerably, ocean acidification caused by increased atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide, and the long-term melting of <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a>, which contributes to sea level rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some large-scale changes could occur <a href="/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change" title="Abrupt climate change">abruptly</a>, i.e., over a short time period, and might also be <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming#Irreversibilities" title="Effects of global warming">irreversible</a>. Examples of abrupt climate change are the rapid release of methane and carbon dioxide from <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, which would lead to amplified global warming, or the <a href="/wiki/Shutdown_of_thermohaline_circulation" title="Shutdown of thermohaline circulation">shutdown of thermohaline circulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-190">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup> Scientific understanding of abrupt climate change is generally poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[187]</a></sup> The probability of abrupt change for some climate related feedbacks may be low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-ccsp_abrupt_climate_change-190">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tar_large_scale_impacts_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-tar_large_scale_impacts-193">[188]</a></sup> Factors that may increase the probability of abrupt climate change include higher magnitudes of global warming, warming that occurs more rapidly, and warming that is sustained over longer time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tar_large_scale_impacts_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-tar_large_scale_impacts-193">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_and_expected_effects_on_social_systems">Observed and expected effects on social systems</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming#Social_systems" title="Effects of global warming">Effects of global warming § Social systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Regional_effects_of_global_warming#Regional_impacts" title="Regional effects of global warming">Regional effects of global warming § Regional impacts</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_national_security" title="Climate change and national security">Climate change and national security</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_humans" title="Effects of climate change on humans">effects of climate change on human systems</a>, mostly due to warming or shifts in <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> patterns, or both, have been detected worldwide. Production of wheat and maize globally has been impacted by climate change. While crop production has increased in some mid-latitude regions such as the UK and Northeast China, economic losses due to extreme weather events have increased globally. There has been a shift from cold- to heat-related mortality in some regions as a result of warming. Livelihoods of <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">indigenous peoples of the Arctic</a> have been altered by climate change, and there is emerging evidence of climate change impacts on livelihoods of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> in other regions. Regional impacts of climate change are now observable at more locations than before, on all continents and across ocean regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>The future social impacts of climate change will be uneven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[190]</a></sup> Many risks are expected to increase with higher magnitudes of global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[191]</a></sup> All regions are at risk of experiencing negative impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup> Low-latitude, less developed areas face the greatest risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> A study from 2015 concluded that economic growth (gross domestic product) of poorer countries is much more impaired with projected future climate warming, than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of 56 studies concluded in 2014 that each degree of temperature rise will increase violence by up to 20%, which includes fist fights, violent crimes, civil unrest or wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>Examples of impacts include:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">Food</a></i>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_productivity" title="Crop productivity">Crop production</a> will probably be negatively affected in low latitude countries, while effects at northern latitudes may be positive or negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[196]</a></sup> Global warming of around 4.6 °C relative to pre-industrial levels could pose a large risk to global and regional food security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[197]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming_on_human_health" title="Effects of global warming on human health">Health</a></i>: Generally impacts will be more negative than positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet2009_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-lancet2009-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet2015_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-lancet2015-205">[200]</a></sup> Impacts include: the effects of extreme weather, leading to injury and loss of life;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[201]</a></sup> and indirect effects, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undernutrition" title="Undernutrition">undernutrition</a> brought on by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_failure" title="Crop failure">crop failures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet2009_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-lancet2009-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet2015_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-lancet2015-205">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[202]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_inundation">Habitat inundation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_humans#Displacement.2Fmigration" title="Effects of climate change on humans">Effects of climate change on humans § Displacement/migration</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_refugee" title="Climate refugee">Climate refugee</a></div>
<p>In small islands and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mega_delta" title="Mega delta">mega deltas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inundation" title="Inundation">inundation</a> as a result of sea level rise is expected to threaten vital infrastructure and human settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[204]</a></sup> This could lead to issues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_refugee" title="Climate refugee">homelessness</a> in countries with low-lying areas such as <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">statelessness</a> for populations in countries such as the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[205]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_global_warming" title="Economics of global warming">Economics of global warming</a></div>
<p>Estimates based on the IPCC A1B emission scenario from additional CO<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub> greenhouse gases released from permafrost, estimate associated impact damages by US$43 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h3>
<p>Continued permafrost degradation will likely result in unstable infrastructure in Arctic regions, or Alaska before 2100. Thus, impacting roads, pipelines and buildings, as well as water distribution, and cause <a href="/wiki/Slope_stability" title="Slope stability">slope failures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_responses_to_global_warming">Possible responses to global warming</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitigation">Mitigation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">Climate change mitigation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_(PBL).png"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1604" data-file-width="1471" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png/300px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png/450px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png/600px-Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_%28PBL%29.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Limiting_global_warming_to_2_degrees_Celsius_-_options_to_reduce_greenhouse_gas_emissions_(PBL).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The graph on the right shows three "pathways" to meet the UNFCCC's 2 °C target, labelled "global technology", "decentralized solutions", and "consumption change". Each pathway shows how various measures (e.g., improved energy efficiency, increased use of renewable energy) could contribute to emissions reductions. Image credit: PBL <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Environmental_Assessment_Agency" title="Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency">Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[208]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mitigation of climate change are actions to reduce greenhouse gas emissions, or enhance the capacity of carbon sinks to absorb GHGs from the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[209]</a></sup> There is a large potential for future reductions in emissions by a combination of activities, including <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">energy conservation</a> and increased <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>; the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-carbon_energy" title="Low-carbon energy">low-carbon energy</a> technologies, such as <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-215">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_mitigation_options_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014_mitigation_options-216">[211]</a></sup> and enhancing carbon sinks through, for example, <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a> and preventing <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-215">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_mitigation_options_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-2014_mitigation_options-216">[211]</a></sup> A 2015 report by <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a> concluded that transitioning to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_economy" title="Low carbon economy">low carbon economy</a> would yield positive return on investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Near- and long-term trends in the global energy system are inconsistent with limiting global warming at below 1.5 or 2 °C, relative to pre-industrial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-progress_to_2_degrees_C_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-progress_to_2_degrees_C-219">[214]</a></sup> Pledges made as part of the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">Cancún agreements</a> are broadly consistent with having a likely chance (66 to 100% probability) of limiting global warming (in the 21st century) at below 3 °C, relative to pre-industrial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-progress_to_2_degrees_C_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-progress_to_2_degrees_C-219">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>In limiting warming at below 2 °C, more stringent emission reductions in the near-term would allow for less rapid reductions after 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[215]</a></sup> Many integrated models are unable to meet the 2 °C target if pessimistic assumptions are made about the availability of mitigation technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptation">Adaptation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming" title="Adaptation to global warming">Adaptation to global warming</a></div>
<p>Other policy responses include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming" title="Adaptation to global warming">adaptation</a> to climate change. Adaptation to climate change may be planned, either in reaction to or anticipation of climate change, or spontaneous, i.e., without government intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[217]</a></sup> Planned adaptation is already occurring on a limited basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-215">[210]</a></sup> The barriers, limits, and costs of future adaptation are not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_215-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-215">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>A concept related to adaptation is <i><a href="/wiki/Adaptive_capacity" title="Adaptive capacity">adaptive capacity</a></i>, which is the ability of a system (human, natural or managed) to adjust to climate change (including climate variability and extremes) to moderate potential damages, to take advantage of opportunities, or to cope with consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[218]</a></sup> Unmitigated climate change (i.e., future climate change without efforts to limit greenhouse gas emissions) would, in the long term, be likely to exceed the capacity of natural, managed and human systems to adapt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>Environmental organizations and public figures have emphasized changes in the climate and the risks they entail, while promoting adaptation to changes in infrastructural needs and emissions reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_engineering">Climate engineering</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">Climate engineering</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">Climate engineering</a> (sometimes called <i>geoengineering</i> or <i>climate intervention</i>) is the deliberate modification of the climate. It has been investigated as a possible response to global warming, e.g. by NASA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[221]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[222]</a></sup> Techniques under research fall generally into the categories <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation_management" title="Solar radiation management">solar radiation management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">carbon dioxide removal</a>, although various other schemes have been suggested. A study from 2014 investigated the most common climate engineering methods and concluded they are either ineffective or have potentially severe side effects and cannot be stopped without causing rapid climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discourse_about_global_warming">Discourse about global warming</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_discussion">Political discussion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_global_warming" title="Politics of global warming">Politics of global warming</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2011_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2011 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2011</a>, <a href="/wiki/2012_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2012 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2012</a>, <a href="/wiki/2013_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2013 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2013</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2015 United Nations Climate Change Conference">2015</a> sessions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="United Nations Climate Change Conference">United Nations Climate Change Conference</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_(vertical)_1.svg"><img alt="refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="457" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg/220px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg/330px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg/440px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_%28vertical%29_1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated_(vertical)_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Article 2 of the UN Framework Convention refers explicitly to "stabilization of greenhouse gas concentrations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[224]</a></sup> To stabilize the atmospheric concentration of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span>, emissions worldwide would need to be dramatically reduced from their present level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[225]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most countries in the world are parties to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[226]</a></sup> The ultimate objective of the Convention is to prevent dangerous human interference of the climate system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[227]</a></sup> As stated in the Convention, this requires that GHG concentrations are stabilized in the atmosphere at a level where <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">ecosystems</a> can adapt naturally to climate change, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">food production</a> is not threatened, and <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_global_warming" title="Economics of global warming">economic development</a> can proceed in a sustainable fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[228]</a></sup> The Framework Convention was agreed on in 1992, but global emissions have risen since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>During negotiations, the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_77" title="Group of 77">G77</a> (a lobbying group in the United Nations representing 133 developing nations)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dessai_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-dessai-235">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> pushed for a mandate requiring developed countries to "[take] the lead" in reducing their emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[231]</a></sup> This was justified on the basis that the developed world's emissions had contributed most to the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Cumulative_and_historical_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">cumulation</a> of GHGs in the atmosphere, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Annual_and_per_capita_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">per-capita emissions</a> (i.e., emissions per head of population) were still relatively low in developing countries, and the emissions of developing countries would grow to meet their development needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-liverman-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:290</sup></p>
<p>This mandate was sustained in the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> to the Framework Convention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liverman_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-liverman-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:290</sup> which entered into legal effect in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyoto_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-kyoto-237">[232]</a></sup> In ratifying the Kyoto Protocol, most developed countries accepted legally binding commitments to limit their emissions. These first-round commitments expired in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyoto_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-kyoto-237">[232]</a></sup> United States President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> rejected the treaty on the basis that "it exempts 80% of the world, including major population centres such as China and India, from compliance, and would cause serious harm to the US economy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dessai_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-dessai-235">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2009_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2009 United Nations Climate Change Conference">15th UNFCCC Conference of the Parties</a>, held in 2009 at <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, several UNFCCC Parties produced the <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Accord" title="Copenhagen Accord">Copenhagen Accord</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150525_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150525-239">[234]</a></sup> Parties associated with the Accord (140 countries, as of November 2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unep_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-unep-240">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup> aim to limit the future increase in global mean temperature to below <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002275149999999999♠</span>2 °C</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[236]</a></sup> The 16th Conference of the Parties (COP16) was held at <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">Cancún</a> in 2010. It produced an agreement, not a binding treaty, that the Parties should take urgent action to reduce greenhouse gas emissions to meet a goal of limiting global warming to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002275149999999999♠</span>2 °C</span> above pre-industrial temperatures. It also recognized the need to consider strengthening the goal to a global average rise of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002274649999999999♠</span>1.5 °C</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[237]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_discussion">Scientific discussion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">Scientific opinion on climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surveys_of_scientists%27_views_on_climate_change" title="Surveys of scientists' views on climate change">Surveys of scientists' views on climate change</a></div>
<p>The discussion continues in scientific articles that are peer-reviewed and assessed by scientists who work in the relevant fields and participate in the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> as of 2013 stated in the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">IPCC Fifth Assessment Report</a> is that it "is extremely likely that human influence has been the dominant cause of the observed warming since the mid-20th century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-243">[238]</a></sup> A 2008 report by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> stated that most scientists by then agreed that observed warming in recent decades was primarily caused by human activities increasing the amount of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrc2008_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-nrc2008-84">[80]</a></sup> In 2005 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> stated that while the overwhelming majority of scientists were in agreement on the main points, some individuals and organizations opposed to the consensus on urgent action needed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions had tried to undermine the science and work of the IPCC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-royal_society_misleading_arguments_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-royal_society_misleading_arguments-244">[239]</a></sup> National science academies have called on world leaders for policies to cut global emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the scientific literature, there is a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">strong consensus that global surface temperatures have increased</a> in recent decades and that the trend is caused mainly by human-induced emissions of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[241]</a></sup> No scientific body of national or international standing <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change#Opposing" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">disagrees with this view</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_MIT_Press_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_MIT_Press-247">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AQAonAAPG_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-AQAonAAPG-248">[243]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discussion_by_the_public_and_in_popular_media">Discussion by the public and in popular media</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">Climate change denial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_controversy" title="Global warming controversy">Global warming controversy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Media_coverage_of_climate_change" title="Media coverage of climate change">Media coverage of climate change</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2233" data-file-width="1662" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg/220px-Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg/330px-Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg/440px-Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ms._magazine_Cover_-_Spring_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global warming was the cover story in this 2007 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Ms._(magazine)" title="Ms. (magazine)">Ms.</a></i> magazine</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_controversy" title="Global warming controversy">global warming controversy</a> refers to a variety of disputes, substantially more pronounced in the <a href="/wiki/Media_coverage_of_climate_change" title="Media coverage of climate change">popular media</a> than in the scientific literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merchants_doubt_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-merchants_doubt-250">[245]</a></sup> regarding the nature, causes, and consequences of global warming. The disputed issues include the causes of increased <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">global average air temperature</a>, especially since the mid-20th century, whether this warming trend is unprecedented or within normal climatic variations, whether humankind has contributed significantly to it, and whether the increase is completely or partially an artefact of poor measurements. Additional disputes concern estimates of climate sensitivity, predictions of additional warming, and what the consequences of global warming will be.</p>
<p>By 1990, <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">American conservative</a> think tanks had begun challenging the legitimacy of global warming as a social problem. They <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">challenged the scientific evidence</a>, argued that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benefits_of_global_warming" title="Benefits of global warming">global warming would have benefits</a>, and asserted that proposed solutions would do more harm than good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[246]</a></sup> Some people dispute aspects of climate change science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-royal_society_misleading_arguments_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-royal_society_misleading_arguments-244">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[247]</a></sup> Organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Competitive_Enterprise_Institute" title="Competitive Enterprise Institute">Competitive Enterprise Institute</a>, conservative commentators, and some companies such as <a href="/wiki/ExxonMobil" title="ExxonMobil">ExxonMobil</a> have challenged IPCC climate change scenarios, funded scientists who disagree with the scientific consensus, and provided their own projections of the economic cost of stricter controls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC_01-12_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC_01-12-255">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[251]</a></sup> On the other hand, some fossil fuel companies have scaled back their efforts in recent years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[252]</a></sup> or even called for policies to reduce global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[253]</a></sup> Global oil companies have begun to acknowledge climate change exists and is caused by human activities and the burning of fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[254]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveys_of_public_opinion">Surveys of public opinion</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Public opinion on climate change">Public opinion on climate change</a></div>
<p>The global warming problem came to international public attention in the late 1980s. Polling groups began to track opinions on the subject, at first mainly in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[255]</a></sup> The longest consistent polling, by <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> in the US, found relatively small deviations of 10% or so from 1998 to 2015 in opinion on the seriousness of global warming, but with increasing polarization between those concerned and those unconcerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first major worldwide poll, conducted by Gallup in 2008–2009 in 127 countries, found that some 62% of people worldwide said they knew about global warming. In the advanced countries of North America, Europe and Japan, 90% or more knew about it (97% in the U.S., 99% in Japan); in less developed countries, especially in Africa, fewer than a quarter knew about it, although many had noticed local weather changes. Among those who knew about global warming, there was a wide variation between nations in belief that the warming was a result of human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[257]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2010, with 111 countries surveyed, Gallup determined that there had been a substantial decrease since 2007–2008 in the number of Americans and Europeans who viewed global warming as a serious threat. In the US, just a little over half the population (53%) viewed it as a serious concern for either themselves or their families; this was 10 points below the 2008 poll (63%). Latin America had the biggest rise in concern: 73% said global warming was a serious threat to their families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[258]</a></sup> This global poll also found that people were more likely to attribute global warming to human activities than to natural causes, except in the US where nearly half (47%) of the population attributed global warming to natural causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>A March–May 2013 survey by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center_for_the_People_%26_the_Press" title="Pew Research Center for the People &amp; the Press">Pew Research Center for the People &amp; the Press</a> polled 39 countries about global threats. According to 54% of those questioned, global warming featured top of the perceived global threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[260]</a></sup> In a January 2013 survey, Pew found that 69% of Americans say there is solid evidence that the Earth's average temperature has gotten warmer over the past few decades, up six points since November 2011 and 12 points since 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 survey of 14 industrialized countries found that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_skeptics" title="Climate change skeptics">skepticism</a> about the danger of global warming was highest in Australia, Norway, New Zealand and the United States, in that order, correlating positively with <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">per capita emissions of carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[262]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>In the 1950s, research suggested increasing temperatures, and a 1952 newspaper reported "climate change". This phrase next appeared in a November 1957 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_of_Northwest_Indiana" title="The Times of Northwest Indiana">The Hammond Times</a></i> which described <a href="/wiki/Roger_Revelle" title="Roger Revelle">Roger Revelle</a>'s research into the effects of increasing human-caused CO<sub>2</sub> emissions on the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>, "a large scale global warming, with radical climate changes may result". Both phrases were only used occasionally until 1975, when <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Smith_Broecker" title="Wallace Smith Broecker">Wallace Smith Broecker</a> published a scientific paper on the topic, "Climatic Change: Are We on the Brink of a Pronounced Global Warming?" The phrase began to come into common use, and in 1976 <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Budyko" title="Mikhail Budyko">Mikhail Budyko</a>'s statement that "a global warming up has started" was widely reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weart_27_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weart_27-268">[263]</a></sup> Other studies, such as a 1971 <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> report, referred to the human impact as "inadvertent climate modification", but an influential 1979 <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> study headed by <a href="/wiki/Jule_Gregory_Charney" title="Jule Gregory Charney">Jule Charney</a> followed Broecker in using <i>global warming</i> for rising surface temperatures, while describing the wider effects of increased CO<sub>2</sub> as <i>climate change</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasacbaon_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasacbaon-269">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986 and November 1987, NASA climate scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Hansen" title="James Hansen">James Hansen</a> gave testimony to Congress on global warming. There were increasing heatwaves and drought problems in the summer of 1988, and when Hansen testified in the Senate on 23 June he sparked worldwide interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weart_Hansen_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weart_Hansen-270">[265]</a></sup> He said, "global warming has reached a level such that we can ascribe with a high degree of confidence a cause and effect relationship between the greenhouse effect and the observed warming."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[266]</a></sup> Public attention increased over the summer, and <i>global warming</i> became the dominant popular term, commonly used both by the press and in public discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasacbaon_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasacbaon-269">[264]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2008 NASA article on usage, <a href="/wiki/Erik_M._Conway" title="Erik M. Conway">Erik M. Conway</a> defined <i>global warming</i> as "the increase in Earth’s average surface temperature due to rising levels of greenhouse gases", while <i>climate change</i> was "a long-term change in the Earth’s climate, or of a region on Earth." As effects such as changing patterns of rainfall and rising sea levels would probably have more impact than temperatures alone, he considered <i>global climate change</i> a more scientifically accurate term, and like the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, the NASA website would emphasize this wider context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasacbaon_269-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasacbaon-269">[264]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 2001 joint statement was signed by the national academies of science of Australia, Belgium, Brazil, Canada, the Caribbean, the People's Republic of China, France, Germany, India, Indonesia, Ireland, Italy, Malaysia, New Zealand, Sweden, and the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The 2005 statement added Japan, Russia, and the U.S. The 2007 statement added Mexico and South Africa. The <a href="/wiki/Network_of_African_Science_Academies" title="Network of African Science Academies">Network of African Science Academies</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Polish Academy of Sciences">Polish Academy of Sciences</a> have issued separate statements. Professional scientific societies include <a href="/wiki/American_Astronomical_Society" title="American Astronomical Society">American Astronomical Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Physics" title="American Institute of Physics">American Institute of Physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Meteorological_Society" title="Australian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society">Australian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Foundation_for_Climate_and_Atmospheric_Sciences" title="Canadian Foundation for Climate and Atmospheric Sciences">Canadian Foundation for Climate and Atmospheric Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Meteorological_and_Oceanographic_Society" title="Canadian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society">Canadian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Arts" title="European Academy of Sciences and Arts">European Academy of Sciences and Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Geosciences_Union" title="European Geosciences Union">European Geosciences Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Science_Foundation" title="European Science Foundation">European Science Foundation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_America" title="Geological Society of America">Geological Society of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_Australia" title="Geological Society of Australia">Geological Society of Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_London" title="Geological Society of London">Geological Society of London</a>-Stratigraphy Commission, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/InterAcademy_Council" title="InterAcademy Council">InterAcademy Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Geodesy_and_Geophysics" title="International Union of Geodesy and Geophysics">International Union of Geodesy and Geophysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Quaternary_Research" title="International Union for Quaternary Research">International Union for Quaternary Research</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.nagt.org/index.html" rel="nofollow">National Association of Geoscience Teachers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council (US)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Meteorological_Society" title="Royal Meteorological Society">Royal Meteorological Society</a>, and <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Earth has already experienced almost 1/2 of the 2.0 °C (3.6 °F) described in the Cancún Agreement. In the last 100 years, Earth's average surface temperature increased by about 0.8 °C (1.4 °F) with about two thirds of the increase occurring over just the last three decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmericasClimateChoices-2011-FullReport_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmericasClimateChoices-2011-FullReport-24">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Scientific journals use "global warming" to describe an increasing global average temperature just at earth's surface, and most of these authorities further limit "global warming" to such increases caused by human activities or increasing greenhouse gases.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The greenhouse effect produces an average worldwide temperature <i>increase</i> of about 33 °C (59 °F) compared to <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> predictions without the greenhouse effect, not an average <i>surface temperature</i> of 33 °C (91 °F). The average worldwide surface temperature is about 14 °C (57 °F).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A rise in temperature from 10 °C to 20 °C is <i>not</i> a doubling of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">absolute temperature</a>; a rise from (273 + 10) K = 283 K to (273 + 20) K = 293 K is an increase of (293–283)/283 = 3.5 %.</span></li>
</ol>

IPCC WGI AR5 (Report). p. 198. <q>Evidence for a warming world comes from multiple independent climate indicators, from high up in the atmosphere to the depths of the oceans. They include changes in surface, atmospheric and oceanic temperatures; glaciers; snow cover; sea ice; sea level and atmospheric water vapour. Scientists from all over the world have independently verified this evidence many times.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.au=Hartmann%2C+D.+L.%3B+Klein+Tank%2C+A.+M.+G.%3B+Rusticucci%2C+M.&amp;rft.btitle=IPCC+WGI+AR5&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=report&amp;rft.pages=198&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.climatechange2013.org%2Fimages%2Freport%2FWG1AR5_Chapter02_FINAL.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span><span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFUnited_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change_.28UNFCCC.292011"><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) (2011). <a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/essential_background/convention/status_of_ratification/items/2631.php" rel="nofollow">"Status of Ratification of the Convention"</a>. UNFCCC Secretariat: <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, Germany: UNFCCC.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.atitle=Status+of+Ratification+of+the+Convention&amp;rft.au=United+Nations+Framework+Convention+on+Climate+Change+%28UNFCCC%29&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fessential_background%2Fconvention%2Fstatus_of_ratification%2Fitems%2F2631.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>. Most countries in the world are Parties to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC), which has adopted the <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002275149999999999♠</span>2 °C</span> limit. As of 25 November 2011, there are 195 parties (194 states and 1 regional economic integration organization (the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>)) to the UNFCCC.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/essential_background/convention/items/6036.php" rel="nofollow">"Introduction to the Convention"</a>. <i>unfccc.int</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2017-08-07</span></span>. <q>Preventing “dangerous” human interference with the climate system is the ultimate aim of the UNFCCC.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.atitle=Introduction+to+the+Convention&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=unfccc.int&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fessential_background%2Fconvention%2Fitems%2F6036.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-unfccc_2_degrees_celsius_target-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-unfccc_2_degrees_celsius_target_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFUnited_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change_.28UNFCCC.292011"><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) (2011). <a class="external text" href="http://unfccc.int/resource/docs/2010/cop16/eng/07a01.pdf#page=2" rel="nofollow">"Conference of the Parties – Sixteenth Session: Decision 1/CP.16: The Cancun Agreements: Outcome of the work of the Ad Hoc Working Group on Long-term Cooperative Action under the Convention (English): Paragraph 4"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. UNFCCC Secretariat: <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, Germany: UNFCCC: 3.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.atitle=Conference+of+the+Parties+%E2%80%93+Sixteenth+Session%3A+Decision+1%2FCP.16%3A+The+Cancun+Agreements%3A+Outcome+of+the+work+of+the+Ad+Hoc+Working+Group+on+Long-term+Cooperative+Action+under+the+Convention+%28English%29%3A+Paragraph+4&amp;rft.au=United+Nations+Framework+Convention+on+Climate+Change+%28UNFCCC%29&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.pages=3&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fresource%2Fdocs%2F2010%2Fcop16%2Feng%2F07a01.pdf%23page%3D2&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> "(...) deep cuts in global greenhouse gas emissions are required according to science, and as documented in the Fourth Assessment Report of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, with a view to reducing global greenhouse gas emissions so as to hold the increase in global average temperature below <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002275149999999999♠</span>2 °C</span> above preindustrial levels"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-AmericasClimateChoices-2011-FullReport-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AmericasClimateChoices-2011-FullReport_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a class="external text" href="http://www.nap.edu/openbook.php?record_id=12781&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow"><i>America's Climate Choices</i></a>. Washington, D.C.: The National Academies Press. 2011. p. 15. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-309-14585-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-309-14585-5">978-0-309-14585-5</a>. <q>The average temperature of the Earth's surface increased by about 1.4 °F (0.8 °C) over the past 100 years, with about 1.0 °F (0.6 °C) of this warming occurring over just the past three decades.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.btitle=America%27s+Climate+Choices&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-309-14585-5&amp;rft.pages=15&amp;rft.place=Washington%2C+D.C.&amp;rft.pub=The+National+Academies+Press&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nap.edu%2Fopenbook.php%3Frecord_id%3D12781%26page%3D1&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation report">Rhein, M.; Rintoul, S.R. (2013). <a class="external text" href="http://www.climatechange2013.org/images/report/WG1AR5_Chapter03_FINAL.pdf" rel="nofollow">"3: Observations: Ocean"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. IPCC WGI AR5 (Report). p. 257. <q>Ocean warming dominates the global energy change inventory. Warming of the ocean accounts for about 93% of the increase in the Earth's energy inventory between 1971 and 2010 (high confidence), with warming of the upper (0 to 700 m) ocean accounting for about 64% of the total. Melting ice (including Arctic sea ice, ice sheets and glaciers) and warming of the continents and atmosphere account for the remainder of the change in energy.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.au=Rhein%2C+M.&amp;rft.au=Rintoul%2C+S.R.&amp;rft.btitle=IPCC+WGI+AR5&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=report&amp;rft.pages=257&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.climatechange2013.org%2Fimages%2Freport%2FWG1AR5_Chapter03_FINAL.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">T. M. L. Wigley (2005). <a class="external text" href="ftp://ftp.soest.hawaii.edu/coastal/Climate%20Articles/Wigley_2005%20Sea%20level%20commitment.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The Climate Change Commitment"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1103934" rel="nofollow">10.1126/science.1103934</a>. <q>Even if atmospheric composition were fixed today, global-mean temperature and sea level rise would continue due to oceanic thermal inertia. These constant-composition (CC) commitments and their uncertainties are quantified. Constant-emissions (CE) commitments are also considered. The CC warming commitment could exceed 1C. The CE warming commitment is 2 to 6C by the year 2400." (...) "A breakdown of the natural and anthropogenic components of the CC commitment, together with uncertainties arising from ocean mixing (Kz) uncertainties, is given in table S1. Past natural forcing (inclusion of which is the default case here) has a marked effect. The natural forcing component is surprisingly large, 64% of the total commitment in 2050, reducing to 52% by 2400.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.au=T.+M.+L.+Wigley&amp;rft.btitle=The+Climate+Change+Commitment&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=ftp%3A%2F%2Fftp.soest.hawaii.edu%2Fcoastal%2FClimate%2520Articles%2FWigley_2005%2520Sea%2520level%2520commitment.pdf&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1126%2Fscience.1103934&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Hegerl-2001-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hegerl-2001_59-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hegerl-2001_59-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Hegerl <i>et al.</i>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/ch9.html" rel="nofollow">Chapter 9: Understanding and Attributing Climate Change</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/ch9s9-4-1-5.html" rel="nofollow">Section 9.4.1.5: The Influence of Other Anthropogenic and Natural Forcings</a>, in <a href="#CITEREFIPCC_AR4_WG12007">IPCC AR4 WG1 2007</a>, pp. 690–691. "Recent estimates indicate a relatively small combined effect of natural forcings on the global mean temperature evolution of the second half of the 20th century, with a small net cooling from the combined effects of solar and volcanic forcings." <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/pdf/assessment-report/ar4/wg1/ar4-wg1-chapter9.pdf" rel="nofollow">p. 690</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFLe_Treut">Le Treut; et al. <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/ch1.html" rel="nofollow">"Chapter 1: Historical Overview of Climate Change Science"</a>. <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/faq-1-2.html" rel="nofollow"><i>FAQ 1.1</i></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+1%3A+Historical+Overview+of+Climate+Change+Science&amp;rft.au=Le+Treut&amp;rft.btitle=FAQ+1.1&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ipcc.ch%2Fpublications_and_data%2Far4%2Fwg1%2Fen%2Fch1.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/pdf/assessment-report/ar4/wg1/ar4-wg1-chapter1.pdf" rel="nofollow">p. 97</a>, in <a href="#CITEREFIPCC_AR4_WG12007">IPCC AR4 WG1 2007</a>: "To emit 240 W m–2, a surface would have to have a temperature of around −19 °C. This is much colder than the conditions that actually exist at the Earth's surface (the global mean surface temperature is about 14 °C). Instead, the necessary −19 °C is found at an altitude about 5 km above the surface."</span></li>

Bond &amp; Haolin Sun, <i>Can Reducing Black Carbon Emissions Counteract Global Warming</i>, ENVIRON. SCI. TECHN. (2005), at 5921 ("BC is the second or third largest individual warming agent, following carbon dioxide and methane."); <i>and</i> J. Hansen, <i>A Brighter Future</i>, 53 CLIMATE CHANGE 435 (2002), <i>available at</i> <a class="external free" href="http://pubs.giss.nasa.gov/docs/2002/2002_Hansen_1.pdf" rel="nofollow">http://pubs.giss.nasa.gov/docs/2002/2002_Hansen_1.pdf</a> (calculating the climate forcing of BC at 1.0±0.5 W/m<sup>2</sup>).</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-IPCC-Def-Of-Certainty-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-IPCC-Def-Of-Certainty_130-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/pdf/assessment-report/ar5/syr/AR5_SYR_FINAL_SPM.pdf" rel="nofollow">"CLIMATE CHANGE 2014: Synthesis Report. Summary for Policymakers"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. IPCC<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 November</span> 2015</span>. <q>The following terms have been used to indicate the assessed likelihood of an outcome or a result: virtually certain 99–100% probability, very likely 90–100%, likely 66–100%, about as likely as not 33–66%, unlikely 0–33%, very unlikely 0–10%, exceptionally unlikely 0–1%. Additional terms (extremely likely: 95–100%, more likely than not &gt;50–100%, more unlikely than likely 0–&lt;50% and extremely unlikely 0–5%) may also be used when appropriate.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGlobal+warming&amp;rft.btitle=CLIMATE+CHANGE+2014%3A+Synthesis+Report.+Summary+for+Policymakers&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=IPCC&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ipcc.ch%2Fpdf%2Fassessment-report%2Far5%2Fsyr%2FAR5_SYR_FINAL_SPM.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFWentz.2CFJ2007">Wentz,FJ; et al.
